Mudabbar After Master's Death: Liability for Accidental Killing
Qawida with furuat and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-mudabbar ba'da mawti mawlahu ka al-mu'tiq fi marad al-mawt' to derive rulings for accidental killing and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
The mudabbar after master's death is analogous to a mukātab in liability, not fully free.

This lesson explores a specific application of a broader qawida: after the master's death, a mudabbar's status shifts to a hybrid of freedom and slavery. The passage presents two scenarios of accidental killing (jinaayah khata') and the dispute between Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for a mufti when assessing blood money (diyah) liability.

Guided Path
1
State the governing qawida
Begin by articulating the rule: 'Al-mudabbar ba'da mawti mawlahu ka al-mu'tiq fi marad al-mawt.' This frames the mudabbar's post-master death status.
The qawida compares the mudabbar to a manumitter in death-illness, implying partial freedom but continued obligations.
2
Identify the two furuat
The text provides two cases: (1) accidental killing during sā‘āyah, (2) accidental killing when the master leaves other property. For each, note the ruling per Abu Hanifah and per the Sahibayn.
In both cases, Abu Hanifah analogizes to the mukātab; the Sahibayn treat him as a free debtor whose ʿāqilah pays diyah.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Abu Hanifah limits the mudabbar's payment to the lesser of his value or diyah, while the Sahibayn impose full diyah on his ʿāqilah. This affects who ultimately bears the burden.
This distinction matters in fatwa when determining whether the victim's family can claim from the mudabbar's earnings or must pursue his ʿāqilah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمدبر بعد موت مولاه كالمعتق في زمن المرض فلو قتل في زمن سعايته خطأ كان عليه الأقل، وعندهما الدية على عاقلته وهي من جنايات المجمع. وصرح أيضًا في الكافي قبيل القسامة بأن المدبر في زمن سعايته كالمكاتب عنده وحرّ مديون عندهما، وكذا لو مات وترك مدبر الآمال له غيره، فقتل هذا المدبر رجلاً خطأ فعليه أن يسعى في قيمته لولي القتيل، عنده كالمكاتب وعندهما عليه الدية. (انتهى).
And the mudabbar after the death of his master is like the manumitter in death illness. So if he kills by mistake in the period of his sā‘āyah, upon him is the lesser [of the two amounts], and according to the two [shaykhs] the blood money is on his ʿāqilah, and it is from the crimes of the group. And it is explicitly stated in al-Kāfī, before the chapter on Qasāmah, that the mudabbar in his sā‘āyah is like a mukātab according to him [Abū Ḥanīfah] and a free indebted person according to the two. And similarly, if a man dies leaving a mudabbar and [also] has other property, and this mudabbar kills a man by mistake, upon him is to work for his value for the guardian of the slain, according to him like a mukātab, and according to the two, upon him is the blood money. (End).
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage continues the previous qāʻidah, extending it to the mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is contingent on the master's death). After the master's death, the mudabbar is like a manumitter in death illness (mu‘tiq fī maraḍ al-mawt) in terms of legal status. The ruling is detailed: if a mudabbar accidentally kills someone during the period of his sā‘āyah (earning to pay required amounts), according to Abū Ḥanīfah, he pays the lesser of two amounts (like the mukātab); according to Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad (the two shaykhs), the blood money is payable by his ʿāqilah (tribal guarantors). The text then cites al-Kāfī, clarifying that in Abū Ḥanīfah's view the mudabbar in sā‘āyah is like a mukātab, while in the view of the two, he is like a free indebted person. A second scenario: if a man dies leaving a mudabbar and other property, and this mudabbar kills a man by mistake, Abū Ḥanīfah holds that the mudabbar must work to pay his own value (qīmah) to the victim's guardian (like a mukātab), while the two say he must pay the full blood money. The passage ends with (end), marking the conclusion.

Key Terms
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is tied to the death of his master (tadbir).
سعاية (sā‘āyah): The period during which a mudabbar (or mukatab) works to earn money to pay the required amounts after the master's death.
عاقلته (ʿāqilatuhu): The tribal group or guarantors who pay blood money on behalf of a free person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Abū Ḥanīfah and the Sahibayn regarding a mudabbar who kills accidentally during sā‘āyah?
A) Both say he pays the lesser amount, like a mukātab
B) Abū Ḥanīfah says he pays the lesser; the Sahibayn say diyah on ʿāqilah
C) Abū Ḥanīfah says diyah on ʿāqilah; the Sahibayn say he pays the lesser
D) Both agree the master's estate is liable

Key Takeaway
The qawida 'al-mudabbar ba'da mawti mawlahu ka al-mu'tiq fi marad al-mawt' is applied to accidental killing, with an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether the mudabbar's liability resembles that of a mukātab or a free debtor.

Heir Cannot Distribute Bequeathed Third When Executor Exists
Ruling on heir's interference in bequest with executor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an heir distributes the bequeathed third in the presence of an executor and apply the underlying Hanafi maxim.

Core Idea
An executor's authority over the bequest is exclusive; the heir's interference is invalid.

The Hanafi school emphasizes the role of the executor (waṣī) as the trustee of the bequest. The heir, even if well-intentioned, cannot override the executor's authority. This short ruling from al-Qunyah illustrates a clear principle: the executor's appointment creates a legal exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Participants
Note the three parties: the heir, the executor (waṣī), and the poor beneficiaries. Recognize that the executor has been appointed by the testator to manage the bequest.
The executor's appointment is a trust (amānah).
2
State the Explicit Ruling
The text says: 'lam yajuz, marrah ukhrā wa yataṣaddaqu bihi' – it is not valid; he must give it again in charity. So the heir's action is null and must be repeated.
The repetition likely requires involving the executor or obtaining proper authority.
3
Extract the Underlying Maxime
The rule implies a maxim: the executor has exclusive right to execute the bequest. The heir cannot substitute his own action for that of the executor.
This maxim falls under the broader principle of 'al-amr bi al-wilāyah' (authority must be exercised by the appointed person).
4
Apply the Maxim to a New Scenario
Consider: if the executor delegates his task to the heir with permission, the heir's distribution becomes valid. This shows the maxim's flexibility based on the executor's consent.
This extrapolation is based on general Hanafi principles of agency (wakālah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوارث إذا تصدق بالثلث الموصى به للفقراء وهناك وصي لم يجز، مرة أخرى ويتصدق به. كما في القنية .
If the heir gives in charity the bequeathed third to the poor while there is an executor, it is not valid; he must give it again and give it in charity. As in al-Qunyah.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This ruling states that if there is an appointed executor (waṣī) for a bequest, the heir cannot unilaterally distribute the bequeathed one-third of the estate to the poor. If the heir does so, the distribution is invalid, and he must repeat the charity properly, presumably through the executor or with proper authority. The source is al-Qunyah, a Hanafi work. This emphasizes the executor's exclusive right to manage the bequest.

Key Terms
الثلث الموصى به (al-thulth al-mūṣā bihi): The one-third of the estate that is bequeathed to someone (here, the poor).
الوصي (al-waṣī): The executor appointed to carry out the bequest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Qunyah, what happens if an heir gives the bequeathed third to the poor while an executor exists?
A) It is valid and sufficient
B) It is invalid and must be done again
C) It is valid only if the heir is also an executor
D) It becomes a gift from the heir's own wealth

Key Takeaway
The executor's authority over the bequest is exclusive; the heir's unilateral charity is invalid and must be repeated.

Executor's Authority to Appoint a Successor
Ruling affirming executor's power to appoint successor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that an executor may appoint a successor and apply it to cases where the executor was appointed by the judge or by the deceased.

Core Idea
An executor can always appoint a successor, regardless of who appointed him.

This short but powerful ruling from the Hanafi tradition confirms that the authority of an executor includes the power to delegate his role to another. The source, al-Khāniyyah, makes no distinction between an executor appointed by the deceased or by the judge. This rule is a cornerstone for the continuity of estate administration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the three parties: the original appointor (deceased or judge), the executor (waṣī), and the successor executor (appointed by the first executor).
The original appointor is not involved in the second appointment.
2
Understand the term 'al-īṣā’'
In this context, al-īṣā’ means the executor's act of appointing a successor. It is not the testator's will but the executor's delegation.
The term is derived from waṣiyya (will) but here it denotes the executor's appointment.
3
Recognize the two scenarios
Note that the executor may have been appointed by the judge (waṣī al-qāḍī) or by the deceased (waṣī al-mayyit). The rule applies equally to both.
No distinction is made; the authority is inherent to the role.
4
Apply the ruling
State clearly: the executor has the legal capacity to appoint a successor. This is a fixed rule in the Hanafi school.
The successor must accept the appointment for it to be effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوصي يملك الإيصاء سواء كان وصي القاضي أو الميت فيها كما في الخانية.
The executor has the authority to make a will [appointing a successor], whether he is the executor of the judge or of the deceased in that, as in al-Khāniyyah.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This is a brief ruling affirming that an executor (waṣī) has the legal capacity to appoint another executor after himself (al-īṣā’), whether the original executor was appointed by the judge (waṣī al-qāḍī) or by the deceased himself. This means the executor can designate a successor to ensure the continuity of managing the estate or bequests. The authority is the same in both cases, as cited from al-Khāniyyah.

Key Terms
الإيصاء (al-īṣā’): The act of appointing an executor in one's own will, or here, an executor appointing a successor.
الوصي (al-waṣī): Executor; a person appointed to manage the estate of a deceased person or to execute a will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can an executor appoint a successor?
A) Only if the executor was appointed by the deceased.
B) Only if the executor was appointed by the judge.
C) Regardless of whether the executor was appointed by the deceased or the judge.
D) Only if the judge permits it.

Key Takeaway
An executor's authority includes the power to appoint a successor, ensuring estate administration can continue without disruption.

Exception to the Rule: The Dead Cannot Acquire Property
Ruling on posthumous acquisition through a hunting net

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exception to the maxim that the dead cannot own property and apply it to analogous cases involving direct causal acts initiated before death.

Core Idea
The dead cannot own, except through a direct cause set in motion during life.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair presents a precise exception to the overarching principle that a deceased person cannot acquire new ownership. It illustrates how Hanafi jurists record such exceptions as dabt (restrictive maxims) to prevent misapplication of the general rule. Here, the act of setting a hunting net before death is deemed a direct cause, and its posthumous result is attributed to the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The text begins with 'الميت لا يملك بعد الموت' — the dead does not own after death. This is the default principle in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Locate the Exception
The word 'إلا' signals an exception: 'except if he set up a net for hunting, then died, and the game is caught in it after death; then he owns it.'
3
Analyze the Causal Link
The net was set during life. The game caught after death is directly attributed to that act. This causal continuity is the basis for the exception.
The cause (setting the net) is a definitive means of acquisition initiated by the deceased.
4
Determine the Legal Consequence
The deceased is deemed to own the game, and it becomes part of his estate, inherited by his heirs.
This is a specific ruling, not a general principle.
5
Note the Source Authority
The ruling is cited from al-Zayla'i, indicating it belongs to the later Hanafi tradition (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
This enhances its authority in the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الميت لا يملك بعد الموت إلا إذا نصب شبكة للصيد ثم مات فتعقل الصيد فيها بعد الموت فإنه يملكه ويورث عنه كذا ذكره الزيلعي
The dead does not own after death, except if he set up a net for hunting, then died, and the game is caught in it after death; then he owns it and it is inherited from him. Thus mentioned al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This is an exceptional ruling in inheritance law. The general principle is that a deceased person cannot acquire ownership after death. However, an exception is made for a net set by the deceased for hunting before death: if game becomes trapped after death, the deceased is considered to own it, and it passes to his heirs. This is because the act of setting the net is a direct cause initiated during life, and the result is attributed to it. The source is al-Zayla'i's commentary.

Key Terms
الشبكة (al-shabakah): A net set for hunting or trapping game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can a deceased person acquire ownership after death according to the passage?
A) If he left a will.
B) If he set a hunting net and game is caught after death.
C) If his heirs make a purchase on his behalf.
D) If he was a martyr.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school makes a specific exception to the rule that the dead cannot own: if the deceased set a hunting net before death, game caught after death is owned by him and inherited.

Inheritable and Non-Inheritable Rights in Hanafi Fiqh
Survey of inheritable and non-inheritable rights according to Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given right as inheritable or non-inheritable according to the Hanafi school, and identify areas of ikhtilaf within the school.

Core Idea
Not all rights transfer to heirs: personal rights and options linked to the person are not inherited; property-related rights are.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents a systematic classification of which rights are inherited and which are not. Understanding this principle is crucial for a mufti when advising on inheritance disputes. The general rule is that physical property (ayan) is always inherited, but rights (huquq) are mixed. This lesson will help you navigate the Hanafi stance on each type of right, including areas of disagreement within the school.

Guided Path
1
Extract the general rule
Identify the overarching principle stated at the beginning: inheritance applies to physical properties (al-a'yan). Then note that rights are subdivided.
The phrase 'الإرث يجري في الأعيان' is the starting maxim.
2
List the non-inherited rights
From the text, enumerate the rights explicitly stated as not inherited: right of pre-emption (shuf'ah), option of condition (khiyar al-shart), hadd of slander (hadd al-qadhf), marriage contract (nikah), agency (wakalah), loans (ariyah), and deposits (wadi'ah).
These are personal or non-proprietary rights.
3
List the inherited and disputed rights
Identify rights that are inherited by consensus: lien (habs al-mabi') , pledge (rahn), blood money (diyah). Then note the disputed ones: option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) and retaliation (qisas). Also note that option of specification (khiyar al-ta'yin) is agreed to vest in the heir initially.
The dispute involves whether the right transfers as inheritance or vests anew in the heir.
4
Analyze the underlying rationale
Consider why some rights are not inherited. Rights that are purely personal (e.g., option of condition, marriage) or penal (hadd) do not transfer. Property-related rights (lien, pledge) do transfer. The disagreement over khiyar al-'ayb centers on whether it is a financial right (thus inheritable) or a personal option.
This helps in deriving rulings for novel cases.
5
Apply the rule to a novel case
Test the principle with a right not mentioned, such as the right of habitation (sukna). Determine whether it resembles inherited rights (property) or non-inherited (personal). Conclude based on analogy.
Use the listed furuat as analogical bases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإرث يجري في الأعيان وأما الحقوق فمنها ما لا يجري فيه كحق الشفعة وخيار الشرط وحد القذف والنكاح لا يورث وحبس المبيع والرهن يورث، والوكالات والعواري والودائع لا تورث واختلفوا في خيار العيب فمنهم من قال يورث، ومنهم من أثبته للوارث ابتداء والدية تورث اتفاقا واختلفوا في القصاص فذكر في الأصل أنه يورث، ومنهم من جعله للورثة ابتداء، ويجوز أن يقال لا يورث عنده خلافا لهما أخذا من مسألة ما لو برهن أحد الورثة على القصاص والباقي غيب فلا بد من إعادته إذا حضروا عنده خلافًا لهما. كذا في آخر اليتيمة. وأما خيار التعيين فاتفقوا أنه يثبت للوارث ابتداء.
Inheritance applies to physical properties. As for rights, some do not apply: like the right of pre-emption, option of condition, hadd of slander, and marriage are not inherited. Lien on the sold object and pledge are inherited. Agencies, loans, and deposits are not inherited. They differed regarding the option of defect: some said it is inherited, others established it for the heir initially. Blood money (diyah) is inherited by agreement. They differed regarding retaliation (qisas): it is mentioned in al-Asl that it is inherited, and some made it for the heirs initially. It is possible to say that it is not inherited according to him, contrary to them, taken from the issue of: if one of the heirs proves retaliation and the rest are absent, it must be repeated when they attend, contrary to them. As in the end of al-Yatima. As for the option of specification, they agreed that it is established for the heir initially.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage systematically lists what is inherited and what is not. The general rule: physical property (ayan) is inherited. Rights are divided: some rights are not inheritable, such as the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah), the option of a condition (khiyar al-shart), the right to enforce hadd for slander (qadhf), and the marriage contract itself. Some rights are inheritable, like the right of lien on sold property (habs al-mabee') and pledge (rahn). Agency, loans (ariyah), and deposits (wadi'ah) are not inherited as they are personal. There is disagreement on the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb): some say it is inherited, others say it vests in the heir initially. Blood money (diyah) is inherited by consensus. For retaliation (qisas), there is a dispute: one position says it is inherited like property, another says it vests in the heirs initially. The text notes a practical implication: if one heir proves qisas while others are absent, the case must be retried when they attend, indicating qisas is not inheritable in the sense of being split. The option of specification (khiyar al-ta'yin) is agreed to vest in the heir directly.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to rescind a sale due to a defect in the item.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation in kind for murder or injury.
حق الشفعة (haqq al-shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to pre-empt a sale.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a contract allowing either party to rescind within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which right is inherited by consensus?
A) Hadd of slander (hadd al-qadhf).
B) Right of pre-emption (shuf'ah).
C) Blood money (diyah).
D) Option of specification (khiyar al-ta'yin).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, physical property is always inherited, but rights are classified: personal and penal rights are not inherited, while financial and security rights are inherited, with some disputed cases like khiyar al-'ayb and qisas.

The Grandfather as Father: Exceptions in Inheritance
Specifying where the grandfather differs from the father in inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the qawida 'al-jadd ka'l-ab' and identify the two faraid exceptions given, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on sibling exclusion.

Core Idea
The grandfather is like the father except for eleven specific cases; five are in inheritance.

One of the foundational principles in Hanafi inheritance is that the paternal grandfather (jadd) takes the place of the father in many rulings. However, this rule is not absolute. The classical legal maxim 'al-jadd ka'l-ab illa fi ihda 'ashrata mas'ala' reminds us that there are exceptions. This lesson dissects two of the five exceptions related to faraid: the case of the paternal grandmother and the exclusion of siblings. It also highlights the internal Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding sibling exclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base qawida
Recognize the statement: 'al-jadd ka'l-ab' (the grandfather is like the father). This is the default rule. Then note the exception clause: 'illa fi ihda 'ashrata mas'ala' (except in eleven issues).
The qawida is a rasm (mnemonic rule) commonly cited in Hanafi inheritance manuals.
2
Categorize the exceptions
The text divides the eleven exceptions into two groups: five in faraid and six in other areas (e.g., testimony, marriage). Focus on the two faraid examples given.
3
Analyze the first exception: the paternal grandmother
Understand that the paternal grandmother (mother of the father) is not blocked by the grandfather from inheriting, unlike her situation when the father is alive.
This is a straightforward exception with no intra-madhab dispute mentioned.
4
Analyze the second exception: sibling exclusion
The father excludes all siblings (full and consanguine). For the grandfather, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa says the grandfather also excludes siblings, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say siblings are not excluded. The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's position.
This is the tamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence): siblings inherit with grandfather according to Sahibayn, but not according to Abu Hanifa.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
Note the phrase 'وعليه الفتوى' – the operative fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view, so in practice, siblings are excluded by the grandfather.
This illustrates how later Hanafi authorities chose one opinion within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجد كالأب إلا في إحدى عشرة مسألة ؛ خمس في الفرائض وست في غيره.
أما الخمس :
فالأولى : الجدَّة أُم الأب لا إرث لها مع الأب ولا تحجب بالجد.
الثانية : الإخوة لأبوين أو لأب يسقطون بالأب ولا يسقطون بالجد على قولهما، ويسقطون به كالأب على قول الإمام وعليه الفتوى فالمخالة على قولهما خاصة.
The grandfather is like the father except in eleven issues: five in faraid and six in other matters.
As for the five:
First: The grandmother, the mother of the father, has no inheritance with the father, and she is not prevented (blocked) by the grandfather.
Second: The siblings from both parents or from the father are dropped by the father and are not dropped by the grandfather according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and they are dropped by him like the father according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and the fatwa is upon his saying, so the exclusion is according to his saying specifically.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the grandfather (father's father) generally occupies the same position as the father in inheritance, but with eleven exceptions. Five of these are in the specific laws of faraid (inheritance shares). The text gives two examples: (1) The paternal grandmother (mother of the father) does not inherit when the father is alive, but if the father is dead and the grandfather is alive, she is not blocked by the grandfather—meaning she still inherits (unlike her position with the father, where she is blocked). (2) Full or consanguine siblings are excluded by the father (they do not inherit when father is present), but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, they are not excluded by the grandfather; however, Abu Hanifa holds that the grandfather excludes them just like the father, and the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view. So the practical fatwa is that siblings are excluded by the grandfather.

Key Terms
الجد (al-jadd): The paternal grandfather (father's father).
المخالة (al-mukhalafah): The exclusion of siblings by the grandfather.
الجدة أم الأب (al-jaddah umm al-ab): The paternal grandmother (mother of the father).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (fatwa), do full siblings inherit when the paternal grandfather is present?
A) Yes, they inherit alongside the grandfather.
B) No, they are excluded by the grandfather.
C) They inherit a fixed share of 1/6.
D) They inherit only if the grandfather is poor.

Key Takeaway
The grandfather is generally treated like the father in Hanafi inheritance, but two key exceptions are that the paternal grandmother is not blocked and that siblings are excluded by the grandfather according to the mufta bihi.

Inheritance of Rights vs. Physical Assets in Hanafi Fiqh
Enumeration of rights that are inheritable or not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Inheritance runs in physical assets' to classify whether a given right is inheritable according to the Hanafi school, and identify the disputed cases.

Core Idea
Not all rights are inherited; only financial rights that replace property are transmissible to heirs.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between physical assets (a'yan) and rights (huquq) when it comes to inheritance. While physical assets are always inherited, rights vary. This lesson examines a key qawida from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair that enumerates which rights are inheritable and which are not, including important intra-madhhab disputes. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis to correctly advise on estate distribution.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Begin by stating the qawida: 'Inheritance runs in physical assets; as for rights, some are not subject to inheritance.' Recognize that this is a sughra (minor) maxim governing inheritance law.
The maxim is kulliya (universal) but has exceptions.
2
Identify the scope
Understand that this maxim applies to inheritance (irth) and covers physical assets (a'yan) and rights (huquq). Rights can be non-inheritable, inheritable, or disputed.
The scope includes faraid, sales, hudud, etc.
3
Work through the furuat
Go through each example in the text: classify each right as inherited or not. Pay attention to the categories: non-inheritable (shuf'a, khiyar al-shart, hadd al-qadhf, nikah, wakala, 'ariya, wadi'a), inheritable (habs al-mabi', rahn, diya), and disputed (khiyar al-'ayb, qisas).
Use the furuat list in the qawida_panel for reference.
4
Analyze the disputes
For khiyar al-'ayb and qisas, note the differing opinions. In the case of qisas, the text mentions a nuanced position of Imam Abu Hanifa (that it is not inherited) contrary to the Sahibayn, based on an example of one heir proving retaliation while others are absent.
This shows the school's internal ikhtilaf and the importance of the mufta bihi.
5
Identify the istithna
Note that blood money (diya) is an exception: it is a right but is inherited by consensus. This refines the maxim: financial rights that replace property are inheritable.
The exception is based on ijma and the nature of diya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإرث يجري في الأعيان وأما الحقوق فمنها ما لا يجري فيه كحق الشفعة وخيار الشرط وحد القذف والنكاح لا يورث وحبس المبيع والرهن يورث، والوكالات والعواري والودائع لا تورث واختلفوا في خيار العيب فمنهم من قال يورث، ومنهم من أثبته للوارث ابتداء والدية تورث اتفاقا واختلفوا في القصاص فذكر في الأصل أنه يورث، ومنهم من جعله للورثة ابتداء، ويجوز أن يقال لا يورث عنده خلافا لهما أخذا من مسألة ما لو برهن أحد الورثة على القصاص والباقي غيب فلا بد من إعادته إذا حضروا عنده خلافًا لهما. كذا في آخر اليتيمة. وأما خيار التعيين فاتفقوا أنه يثبت للوارث ابتداء.
Inheritance runs in physical assets. As for rights, some of them are not inherited, like the right of pre-emption, option of stipulation, penalty of slander, and marriage is not inherited. The right to retain the sold item and pledge are inherited. Agencies, loans, and deposits are not inherited. And they differed regarding the option of defect: some said it is inherited, some said it is established for the heir initially. The blood money (diya) is inherited by agreement. And they differed regarding retaliation (qisas): it is mentioned in al-Asl that it is inherited, and some make it for the heirs initially. And it may be said that it is not inherited in his view contrary to both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), taken from the issue of if one heir proves retaliation while others are absent, then it must be repeated when they are present, contrary to them. Thus in the end of al-Yatimah. As for the option of specification, they agreed that it is established for the heir initially.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage lays down a qawida that inheritance primarily applies to physical assets ('ayn). Rights (huquq) vary: some are inheritable, some are not, and some are disputed. The text lists examples. Non-inheritable rights include: shuf'a (pre-emption), khiyar al-shart (option of condition), hadd al-qadhf (penalty for false accusation of adultery), and marriage (the marital bond itself is not inherited, though dowry may be). Inheritable rights include: habs al-mabi' (right to retain sold item if unpaid) and rahn (pledge). Non-inheritable: wakalat (agency), ariya (loan), wadi'a (deposit). Khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) is disputed: some say it is inherited, others say it vests in the heir directly. Diya (blood money) is unanimously inherited. Qisas (retaliation) is disputed; the text mentions a nuance about proof when some heirs are absent. Khiyar al-ta'yin (option of specification) is agreed to vest in the heir directly. This detailed enumeration shows the furuat of the maxim.

Key Terms
أعيان (a'yan): Physical assets or property
حقوق (huquq): Rights, as opposed to physical assets

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Blood money (diya)
B) Right of pre-emption (shuf'a)
C) Pledge (rahn)
D) Retaliation (qisas) according to some

Key Takeaway
Inheritance primarily applies to physical assets; rights are inheritable only if they are financial in nature and explicitly recognized by the school.

Grandfather Compared to Father: Thirteen Exceptions
Exceptions where grandfather differs from father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-jadd ka al-ab illa fi...' and identify the specific exceptions where the grandfather differs from the father, applying the mufta bihi positions.

Core Idea
The grandfather is like the father except in thirteen specific rulings.

This passage presents a well-known Hanafi qawida: the grandfather (paternal) is generally treated like the father, but with a finite set of exceptions. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for a mufti when issuing fatwas on inheritance, family law, and other areas. The text lists five inheritance exceptions and six in other chapters, then adds two more from the author's research, totaling thirteen. Within these, there are intra-madhhab differences; the fatwa is generally on the view of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Maxim
The core rule is analogy between grandfather and father. Commit the maxim 'al-jadd ka al-ab illa...' to memory. This is the foundation.
The exceptions are finite and must be learned precisely.
2
Categorize the Exceptions
Separate the five inheritance issues (faraid) from the six (then two) non-inheritance issues. This helps organize recall.
The inheritance exceptions involve grandmother, brothers, mother's share, wala', etc.
3
Identify Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilaf
Note where Imam Abu Hanifa differs from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn). For example, on exclusion of brothers by grandfather and mother's share.
The mufta bihi (fatwa position) is on Imam's view in these differences.
4
Apply the Mufta Bihi
When issuing fatwa, use Imam's rulings (e.g., brothers excluded by grandfather, mother gets 1/3 of whole) unless a later tarjih overrides.
Always check the source authority; these exceptions are from well-known books.
5
Extend to New Cases via Qawida
For a new scenario not listed, test if it falls under the general rule (grandfather = father) or is analogous to an exception.
Use the list of exceptions as a touchstone; qiyas should be applied carefully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجد كالأب إلا في إحدى عشرة مسألة ؛ خمس في الفرائض وست في غيره.
أما الخمس :
فالأولى : الجدَّة أُم الأب لا إرث لها مع الأب ولا تحجب بالجد.
الثانية : الإخوة لأبوين أو لأب يسقطون بالأب ولا يسقطون بالجد على قولهما، ويسقطون به كالأب على قول الإمام وعليه الفتوى فالمخالة على قولهما خاصة.
الثالثة : للأم ثلث ما بقي مع أحد الزوجين والأب، ولو كان مكان الأب جدّ المال عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله خلافا لأبي يوسف رحمه الله فللأم ثلث جميع المال.
الرابعة : لو مات المعتق عن أب معتقه وابن معتقه فللأب السدس والباقي في رواية ولو كان مكان الأب جدّ فالكل للابن في الروايات كلها على قول الإمام .
الخامسة : لو ترك جدّ معتقه وأخاه ؛ قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله يختص الجد بالولاء، وقالا الولاء بينهما ولو كان مكان الجد أب فالميراث كله له اتفاقا .
وأما المسائل الست؛ فأربع في الكتب المشهورة :
۱ - لو أوصى لأقرباء فلان لا يدخل الأب ويدخل الجد في ظاهر الرواية 
۲ - وفي صدقة الفطر تجب صدقة فطر الولد على أبيه الغني دون جده.
٣ - ولو أعتق الأب جرّ ولاء ولده إلى مواليه دون الجد.
٤ - ويصير الصغير مسلمًا بإسلام أبيه دون جده.
ه - لو مات وترك أولادًا صغارًا ومالاً فالولاية للأب فهو كوصي الميت بخلاف الجد.
٦ - في ولاية الإنكاح لو كان للصغير أخ وجد ؛ فعلى قول أبي يوسف رحمه الله يشتركان وعلى قول الإمام رحمه الله يختص الجد. ولو كان مكانه أب اختص اتفاقا .
ثم زدت أخرى وهي أنه إذا مات أبوه صار يتيما ولا يقوم الجد مقام الأب لإزالة اليتيم عنه . فهي اثنتا عشرة مسألة . ثم رأيت أخرى في نفقات الخانية؛ لو مات وترك أولادًا صغارًا ولا مال له ولهم أم وجد أب الأب فالنفقة عليهما أثلاثا؛ الثلث على الأم والثلثان على الجد .(انتهى). ولو كان الأب كانت كلها عليه ولا تشاركه الأم في نفقتهم. فهي ثلاث عشرة.
The grandfather is like the father except in eleven issues: five in faraid (inheritance) and six in others. As for the five: First: the grandmother mother of the father has no inheritance with the father and is not blocked by the grandfather. Second: full or consanguine brothers are excluded by the father but not by the grandfather according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and they are excluded by him like the father according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) and the fatwa is upon that, so the exclusion is according to their view only. Third: For the mother, one-third of what remains with one of the spouses and the father, and if instead of the father there is a grandfather, the wealth (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf) is that the mother gets one-third of all the property. Fourth: If a freedman dies leaving the father of his liberator and the son of his liberator, then the father gets one-sixth and the rest in one narration; and if instead of the father there is a grandfather, then the whole property goes to the son in all narrations according to Imam. Fifth: If he leaves the grandfather of his liberator and his brother, Abu Hanifa said the grandfather takes the wala' exclusively, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said the wala' is between them; and if instead of the grandfather there was a father, the whole inheritance goes to him by agreement. As for the six issues: four are in the well-known books: 1) If one makes a will to the relatives of so-and-so, the father does not enter but the grandfather enters in the apparent narration. 2) In sadaqat al-fitr, the father must pay fitr for his child if he is wealthy, but not the grandfather. 3) If the father frees a slave, he draws the wala' of his child to his own patrons, unlike the grandfather. 4) A minor becomes Muslim by his father's Islam, not by his grandfather's. 5) If he dies and leaves minor children and wealth, guardianship belongs to the father; he is like the executor of the deceased, unlike the grandfather. 6) In the guardianship of marriage, if the minor has a brother and a grandfather, according to Abu Yusuf they both share; according to Imam, the grandfather is exclusively entitled. And if the father were there, he is exclusively entitled by agreement. Then I added another: if his father dies, he becomes an orphan, and the grandfather does not take the place of the father in removing the orphan status from him. So that makes twelve issues. Then I saw another in Nafaqat al-Khaniyyah: if a man dies leaving minor children and no wealth, and they have a mother and a grandfather (father's father), the maintenance is upon both in thirds: one-third on the mother, two-thirds on the grandfather. (End). And if the father were alive, it would all be on him and the mother would not share in their maintenance. So that makes thirteen.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a classic qawida with furuat from the Hanafi school. The maxim is: 'The grandfather (sahih, i.e., father's father) is analogous to the father in most rulings of inheritance and other areas, except in a specific set of issues.' The text identifies eleven original exceptions: five related to inheritance (faraid) and six in other areas of fiqh. The author then adds two more from his own research (one from orphan status, one from maintenance), totaling thirteen. The inheritance exceptions include the grandmother's position, exclusion of siblings, the mother's share, wala' distribution, and bequest interpretation. The non-inheritance exceptions include will interpretation, fitr obligation, wala' upon freeing, Islam of minors, guardianship of property, and marriage guardianship. The additional ones involve orphan status and maintenance. The text shows that the grandfather is not completely equivalent to the father in all secondary rulings. The fatwa is generally on the view of Imam Abu Hanifa where he differs. This is a fundamental passage for understanding the role of the grandfather in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الجد (al-jadd): Paternal grandfather (father's father), also called جد صحيح
المخالة (al-mukhalah): Possibly a corruption; likely 'المخالفة' meaning 'the difference' or 'the exclusion'

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Eleven
B) Twelve
C) Thirteen
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The grandfather (paternal) is treated like the father except in thirteen specific rulings, which a mufti must know to issue correct fatwas.

Executor of the Deceased Compared to the Father: The First Exception
Exceptions where executor differs from father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule that the executor is like the father, identify the first exception concerning lending, and apply the rule to new scenarios.

Core Idea
The executor's powers mirror the father's except where specified — lending is a key exception.

In Hanafi fiqh, the executor (wasi) of a deceased person generally holds the same authority over the estate as a father does over his children. However, there are specific exceptions where the two differ. This lesson focuses on the first of those exceptions: the permissibility of lending money from the estate. Understanding this nuance is crucial for muftis addressing queries about executor powers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
Recognize the principle: 'وصي الميت كالأب' (the executor is like the father). This is a general analogy.
The base rule implies the executor inherits all of the father's authority unless an exception is stated.
2
Recognize the exception structure
The phrase 'إلا في مسائل' (except in certain issues) indicates specific departures from the analogy.
The text only lists one exception here, but the structure implies there are more in the complete work.
3
Examine the first exception
Read the exception: lending money. For the executor, it is permissible by agreement (اتفاقا). For the father, it is permissible only in one narration (في رواية).
'في رواية' indicates a weaker transmission, meaning the stronger view is that the father may not lend without restriction.
4
Interpret the legal nuance
The executor's lending is more firmly established (by agreement) than the father's, which is disputed. Thus, in this case, the executor has more freedom.
This does not mean the executor generally has broader powers; the rule is equality with specific exceptions.
5
Apply the qawida
For any matter not listed as an exception, the executor follows the father's rulings. So in selling, marrying off minors, etc., the executor's authority matches the father's.
The mufti must check the full list of exceptions from the source text before issuing a fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصي الميت كالأب إلا في مسائل : الأولى : يجوز إقراضه اتفاقا ويجوز إقراض الاب في رواية .
The executor of the deceased is like the father except in issues: First: It is permissible to lend to him by agreement, and it is permissible to lend to the father in one narration.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage introduces another qawida: the executor (wasi) of a deceased person is analogous to the father in his authority over the estate and children, but with some exceptions. The text only gives the first exception here: borrowing/lending. The ruling is that it is permissible for an executor to lend money from the estate by agreement among scholars. As for the father, there is a difference: in one narration (riwaya) it is permissible for him to lend, but apparently there is another view. This is a brief excerpt; the book likely lists more exceptions, but only the first is given on this page. The passage sets up a comparative rule between the executor and the father's powers.

Key Terms
وصي الميت (wasi al-mayyit): The executor appointed by the deceased to manage the estate

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The executor's authority is generally equal to the father's, but lending is one clearly established exception.

Thirteen Differences Between Father and Grandfather
A list of thirteen rulings distinguishing the grandfather from the father in Hanafi law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the thirteen specific fiqh rulings where the grandfather is distinguished from the father in Hanafi law and apply the underlying principle to new family law cases.

Core Idea
The grandfather is not the father: thirteen rulings show where his authority is limited or absent.

In Hanafi fiqh, the grandfather often steps into the father's role, but the law draws clear lines. This list of thirteen issues—collected from various sources like al-Khaniyya—highlights when the grandfather's power differs. Understanding these distinctions is essential for a mufti handling family disputes, guardianship, and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recognize that the grandfather is generally treated like the father in many rulings, but not in all. The text enumerates specific exceptions.
This is a qawida with furuat: the maxim is 'grandfather is like father except in these matters.'
2
List the thirteen exceptions by area
Group the issues into categories: inheritance (issues 1-3), wala (4-5), wills (1), sadaqat al-fitr (2), emancipation (3), conversion (4), guardianship (5-6), orphanhood (7), maintenance (8).
Use the Arabic text to verify each ruling.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi disputes
Note the ikhtilaf in issue 3 (mother's share) and issue 5 (wala distribution). Understand the different positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
The thamarah al-khilaf affects actual inheritance and wala outcomes.
4
Consider the underlying rationale
The father has a direct, primary relationship; the grandfather's authority is secondary, often shared or contingent. This explains why obligations like sadaqat al-fitr fall exclusively on the father.
This principle guides muftis in analogical reasoning.
5
Apply to new cases
When a new scenario involves a grandfather and father, check against these thirteen to see if the grandfather's role is identical or distinct.
Use the list as a checklist for fatwa formulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : للأم ثلث ما بقي مع أحد الزوجين والأب، ولو كان مكان الأب جدّ المال عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله خلافا لأبي يوسف رحمه الله فللأم ثلث جميع المال للابن. الرابعة : لو مات المعتق عن أب معتقه وابن معتقه فللأب السدس والباقي في رواية ولو كان مكان الأب جدّ فالكل للابن في الروايات كلها على قول الإمام . الخامسة : لو ترك جدّ معتقه وأخاه ؛ قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله يختص الجد بالولاء، وقالا الولاء بينهما ولو كان مكان الجد أب فالميراث كله له اتفاقا . وأما المسائل الست؛ فأربع في الكتب المشهورة : ۱ - لو أوصى لأقرباء فلان لا يدخل الأب ويدخل الجد في ظاهر الرواية ۲ - وفي صدقة الفطر تجب صدقة فطر الولد على أبيه الغني دون جده. ٣ - ولو أعتق الأب جرّ ولاء ولده إلى مواليه دون الجد. ٤ - ويصير الصغير مسلمًا بإسلام أبيه دون جده. ه - لو مات وترك أولادًا صغارًا ومالاً فالولاية للأب فهو كوصي الميت بخلاف الجد. ٦ - في ولاية الإنكاح لو كان للصغير أخ وجد ؛ فعلى قول أبي يوسف رحمه الله يشتركان وعلى قول الإمام رحمه الله يختص الجد. ولو كان مكانه أب اختص اتفاقا . ثم زدت أخرى وهي أنه إذا مات أبوه صار يتيما ولا يقوم الجد مقام الأب لإزالة اليتيم عنه . فهي اثنتا عشرة مسألة . ثم رأيت أخرى في نفقات الخانية؛ لو مات وترك أولادًا صغارًا ولا مال له ولهم أم وجد أب الأب فالنفقة عليهما أثلاثا؛ الثلث على الأم والثلثان على الجد .(انتهى). ولو كان الأب كانت كلها عليه ولا تشاركه الأم في نفقتهم. فهي ثلاث عشرة.
Third: For the mother is one-third of what remains with one of the spouses and the father, and if in place of the father there were a grandfather, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) contrary to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him), then for the mother is one-third of the entire estate, for the son. Fourth: If a freed slave dies leaving the father of his emancipator and a son of his emancipator, then for the father is one-sixth and the remainder in one narration; and if in place of the father there were a grandfather, then everything is for the son in all narrations according to the Imam's statement. Fifth: If one leaves the grandfather of his emancipator and his brother; Abu Hanifa said the grandfather is exclusively entitled to the wala, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said the wala is between them; and if in place of the grandfather there were a father, the entire inheritance is for him by agreement. As for the six issues: four are in the famous books: 1 - If one makes a bequest to the relatives of so-and-so, the father does not enter but the grandfather enters according to the apparent narration. 2 - Regarding sadaqat al-fitr, the sadaqat al-fitr of the child is obligatory on his wealthy father but not on his grandfather. 3 - If the father emancipates, he draws the wala of his child to his own clients, not the grandfather. 4 - The minor becomes Muslim by his father's Islam, not by his grandfather's. 5 - If a man dies leaving minor children and wealth, guardianship is for the father, and he is like the executor of the deceased, unlike the grandfather. 6 - Regarding marriage guardianship, if a minor has a brother and a grandfather, according to Abu Yusuf they share, and according to the Imam the grandfather is exclusively entitled; and if in his place there were a father, he is exclusively entitled by agreement. Then I added another: that if his father dies, he becomes an orphan, and the grandfather does not take the place of the father to remove the orphan status from him. So that is twelve issues. Then I saw another in al-Khaniyya on maintenance: if a man dies leaving minor children and no wealth, and they have a mother and a paternal grandfather, then maintenance is on them in thirds: one-third on the mother and two-thirds on the grandfather. (End). And if it were the father, it would all be on him and the mother would not share in their maintenance. So that is thirteen.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed list of thirteen rulings where the grandfather (jadd) is distinguished from the father (ab) in Hanafi fiqh. The rulings span inheritance (first three), wills, sadaqat al-fitr, wala, conversion of minors, guardianship, marriage guardianship, orphanhood, and maintenance. The author collects these from various sources, including the famous books and additions from al-Khaniyya. Notable is the ikhtilaf in the fifth ruling: Abu Hanifa gives exclusive wala to the grandfather over the brother, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad split it, which affects inheritance distribution. The list underscores the principle that the grandfather, while often treated similarly to the father, has a distinct legal status with specific exceptions.

Key Terms
جد (jadd): Paternal grandfather (father's father), distinct from the father in certain rulings.
أب (ab): Father, the primary male guardian in family and financial matters.
ولاء (wala): The bond of clientage between a freed slave and his emancipator, affecting inheritance.

The Executor vs. the Father: Eleven Key Exceptions
Listing of eleven cases where executor differs from father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eleven exceptions where an executor differs from a father and apply the qawida 'al-wasi ka al-ab illa fi masail' to new cases involving guardianship of minors.

Core Idea
The executor's authority mirrors the father's except where explicitly restricted — knowing the exceptions prevents overstepping.

In Hanafi law, the executor (wasi) of a deceased person is generally treated like the father (al-ab) in terms of authority over the minor's wealth and person. However, this rule has eleven important exceptions. These exceptions limit the executor's powers in financial transactions, personal care, and guardianship. Understanding these restrictions is crucial for a mufti when advising on matters of orphans' property and family law.

Guided Path
1
State the General Maxim
Identify the qawida: 'The executor of the deceased is like the father except in certain issues.' This establishes a default rule of similarity but signals exceptions.
The exceptions are enumerated as eleven specific cases.
2
Work Through the Furuat Systematically
For each exception, identify the issue (e.g., lending, debt payment, marriage), then compare the ruling for the executor vs. the father. Note the wording: 'bikhilaf al-ab' (unlike the father).
Pay special attention to conditions: e.g., executor may lend by agreement, father only in one narration (riwaya).
3
Identify the Underlying Principle
The father has inherent wilaya (guardianship) by nature; the executor's authority is derivative and limited. Hence, the father can benefit from the child's wealth in need, while the executor can only take a wage.
This explains restrictions on debt payment, eating, pawning, and marriage.
4
Apply the Maxim to a New Scenario
Given a new case (e.g., executor gifting child's property), determine if it is similar to an enumerated exception or falls under the general rule.
Use qiyas if not explicitly listed; consult the underlying principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصي الميت كالأب إلا في مسائل : الأولى : يجوز إقراضه اتفاقا ويجوز إقراض الاب في رواية . الثانية : يبيع ويشتري لنفسه بشرط الخيرية لليتيم وللأب ذلك بشرط ألا ضرر . الثالثة : للأب أن يقضي دينه من مال ولده بخلاف الوصي. الرابعة : للأب الأكل من مال ولده عند الحاجة، وللوصي بقدر عمله. الخامسة : للأب أن يرهن مال ولده على دينه بخلاف الوصي. السادسة : لا تقوم عبارته مقام عبارتين فإذا باع أو اشترى لنفسه بالشرط فلا بد من قوله قبلت بعد الإيجاب بخلاف الأب. السابعة : لا يلي الإنكاح بخلاف الأب. الثامنة : لا يمونه بخلاف الأب. التاسعة : لا يؤدي من ماله صدقة فطره بخلاف الأب. العاشرة : لا يستخدمه بخلاف الأب. الحادية عشرة : لا حضانة له بخلاف الأب.
The executor of the deceased is like the father except in the following issues: First: It is permissible for him to lend by agreement, and it is permissible for the father in one narration. Second: He sells and buys for himself on condition of benefit for the orphan, and for the father that is on condition of no harm. Third: The father may pay his debt from his child's wealth, unlike the executor. Fourth: The father may eat from his child's wealth when in need, and the executor may only take a wage for his work. Fifth: The father may pledge his child's wealth for his own debt, unlike the executor. Sixth: His statement does not stand in place of two statements; so if he sells or buys for himself with a condition, he must say 'I accept' after the offer, unlike the father. Seventh: He does not conduct marriage, unlike the father. Eighth: He does not provide maintenance, unlike the father. Ninth: He does not pay sadaqat al-fitr from his own wealth, unlike the father. Tenth: He does not employ him, unlike the father. Eleventh: He does not have custody, unlike the father.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage outlines the general rule that an executor (wasi) of a deceased has legal powers similar to the father over the deceased's children and wealth, but then lists eleven exceptions where the two differ. These exceptions cover financial matters (lending, selling, debt payment, eating from the child's wealth, pawning), contractual formalities (need for explicit acceptance), and personal matters (marriage guardianship, maintenance, sadaqat al-fitr, employment, custody). The underlying principle is that the father has inherent authority (wilaya) while the executor's authority is derivative and more restricted. For example, a father can use his child's wealth to pay his own debts, but an executor cannot. The executor's scope is limited to what benefits the orphans and compensates him for his work.

Key Terms
وصي الميت (wasi al-mayyit): The person appointed by the deceased to manage his estate and care for minor children.
الأب (al-ab): The father, who has inherent guardianship over his children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Maxim of Forgetfulness (Nisyan): Scope and Exceptions
Expounding the legal maxim of nisyan with its furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim on forgetfulness to distinguish cases where forgetfulness removes sin/liability from those where worldly rulings remain, using the principle of reminder and motive.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness removes sin but does not erase worldly obligations; the presence of a reminder and motive determines the ruling.

The hadith 'Allah has lifted from my ummah error, forgetfulness, and compulsion' is a cornerstone of Hanafi legal theory. However, its application is nuanced: it removes sin (otherworldly) but does not automatically nullify all worldly consequences. The Hanafi jurists have derived a rich set of rules and exceptions, guided by the presence or absence of a reminder and motive. This lesson unpacks the maxim, its usuli foundation, and its practical applications across various chapters of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and its Dalil
Locate the hadith in the text and the usuli analysis that limits its scope to otherworldly rulings only. Understand the Hanafi argument that 'forgetfulness' (mushtarak) has no generality, so the hadith only covers sin.
The hadith is hasan, so its thubut is zanni.
2
Distinguish Commanded vs. Prohibited Acts
Classify the cases: for missed obligatory acts, forgetfulness necessitates qada (make-up) without sin; for prohibited acts, forgetfulness creates a doubt in penalty (shubha).
The text says 'إذا وقع في ترك مأمور لم يسقط بل يجب تداركه'.
3
Apply the Principle of Reminder and Motive
Use the rule from al-Tahrir: if a reminder is present (e.g., being in prayer) and no motive (e.g., hunger), the excuse fails; if a motive (e.g., hunger in fasting) and no reminder, the excuse applies; if neither, even more so.
This explains why eating in prayer invalidates, but eating in fasting does not.
4
Work Through the Furuat
Trace each case mentioned in the text—forgetting prayer, fasting, zakat, etc., and note how the ruling follows the maxim: qada required, no sin. Then note exceptions like divorce, emancipation, and salam in prayer where forgetfulness has the same effect as intentional.
These exceptions are based on the nature of the act (e.g., speech acts) or the presence of a reminder.
5
Identify the Exceptions and Their Reasons
List the cases where forgetfulness does not change the ruling: divorce, emancipation, oaths, salam after two rak'ahs, eating in prayer. Understand the rationale: either the act is heavily based on intention, or a reminder was present (being in prayer).
The text says 'والناسي والعامد في اليمين سواء'.

Rulings on the forgetful person: The definition of forgetfulness in al-Tahrir is that it is not remembering something at the time of need. They differed on the difference between forgetfulness (sahw) and nisyan, and the relied-upon is that they are synonymous. The scholars agreed that it entirely removes sin, due to the hasan hadith: 'Indeed Allah has lifted from my ummah error, forgetfulness, and what they are compelled to do.' The usulis said: This is from the category of leaving the literal meaning by indication of the context of speech, because the actual error and its two companions are not removed; so what is intended is its ruling, which is of two types: otherworldly, which is sin, and worldly, which is invalidation. The two rulings are different, so the ruling, after being metaphorical, is shared and thus does not encompass. No. According to us (Hanafis), because the shared term has no generality, and according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, because the metaphorical has generality. So when the otherworldly is established by consensus, the other is not established, as in al-Tanqih, and its completion is in our commentary on al-Manar. As for the worldly ruling: if it occurs in abandoning a commanded act, it is not dropped but must be made up, and the reward for it is not obtained; or if it is a prohibited act that entails a penalty, it is a doubt in dropping it. So whoever forgets a prayer, fast, pilgrimage, zakat, expiation, or vow must make it up without fear. Likewise, standing at other than 'Arafa by mistake requires making up by consensus. Among them: one who prays with an impure substance that prevents validity forgetfully, or forgets a pillar of the prayer, or is certain of error in ijtihad regarding water and garment at the time of prayer and fasting, or forgets the intention of fasting, or speaks in prayer forgetfully. Among what drops its ruling due to forgetfulness: if one eats or drinks forgetfully while fasting, or has intercourse, it does not invalidate; if one eats forgetfully in prayer, it invalidates. If one says the salam forgetfully in a four-rak'ah prayer after two rak'ahs, the forgetful and intentional are the same in oaths. Likewise in divorce if he says 'my wife is divorced' while forgetful that he has a wife; likewise in emancipation; likewise in prohibitions of ihram. It has been given a principle in al-Tahrir: if there is a reminder with him and no motive, like the eating of the one in prayer, it does not drop due to his negligence, unlike his salam in the sitting; or if there is no reminder with a motive, like the eating of the fasting person, it drops; or if neither, then more so, like the forgetting of the slaughterer to say tasmiyah. (Ended). From the issues of forgetfulness: if the debtor forgets the debt until he dies, if it is the price of a sale or a loan, he is not held accountable; if it is usurpation, he is held accountable, as in al-Khaniyyah. Or from them: if the executor knows that the testator made bequests but forgot their amounts, its ruling is in the bequests of Khizanat al-Muftiyin.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the legal maxim of forgetfulness (nisyan) based on the hadith, and then lists numerous applications across different chapters of fiqh. The author begins by defining nisyan as 'not remembering something at the time of need' and notes that the relied-upon view equates sahw and nisyan. The core of the discussion is the hadith 'Allah has lifted from my ummah error, forgetfulness, and compulsion.' The usulis explain that this lifting applies only to the ruling (sin), not to the actual act. The ruling is divided into otherworldly (sin) and worldly (invalidation). According to the Hanafis, since the term 'forgetfulness' is shared (mushtarak), it does not convey generality; thus the hadith only covers the otherworldly aspect by consensus, leaving worldly rulings to be determined separately. The worldly ruling: if a commanded act is omitted, it must be made up (qada) and no reward is earned; if a prohibited act is done, any penalty becomes doubtful. The text then lists many cases: forgetting prayers, fasts, etc., requires qada; praying with impurity forgetfully, forgetting a pillar, etc., also require repetition. Some rulings are dropped due to forgetfulness: eating/drinking forgetfully in Ramadan does not break the fast, but eating forgetfully in prayer does invalidate. Salam forgetfully after two rak'ahs is treated like intentional, etc. A principle is given: if there is a reminder and no motive (e.g., eating while praying), the excuse does not stand; if there is a motive but no reminder (e.g., eating while fasting), it drops; if neither, it's even more likely to drop (e.g., forgetting tasmiyah while slaughtering). Finally, two additional issues: a debtor who forgets a debt is not liable if it is a sale or loan, but is liable if it is usurpation; and an executor who forgets the amount of bequests has special rulings in Khizanat al-Muftiyin.

Key Terms
النسيان (al-nisyān): Not remembering something at the time of need; synonymous with sahw according to the relied-upon view.
السهو (al-sahw): Forgetfulness; used synonymously with nisyan in this text.
مُسقط للإثم (musqit lil-ithm): Removes sin entirely (from the hadith).

Key Takeaway
The maxim of forgetfulness removes sin but not worldly obligations; the presence of a reminder and motive determines the exceptions.

Forgetfulness as a Legal Excuse: The Hanafi Principle of Nisyān
Exposition of the hadith on forgetfulness and its legal implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that forgetfulness removes only sin (ithm) and not worldly legal consequences, and identify exceptions based on the presence of a reminder (mudhakkir) and compelling reason (dā'iyah).

Core Idea
Forgetfulness removes sin, not worldly obligations unless no reminder and compelling reason.

The hadith 'Allah has lifted error, forgetfulness, and coercion from my Ummah' is often taken as a blanket excuse. However, Hanafi usul scholars distinguish: the hadith only removes otherworldly sin; worldly rulings like the obligation to make up missed acts or the invalidity of certain acts remain. This lesson unpacks that principle and its application across a range of cases, providing a framework for muftis to navigate forgetfulness as a legal excuse.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two realms of ruling
Identify that the hadith's removal applies to sin (ithm, ukhrawi) not to worldly rulings (hukm dunyawi). Recognize that the word 'hukm' in the hadith becomes homonymous (mushtarak) once taken metaphorically, and Hanafis say mushtarak has no generality.
This is based on the usul principle that a homonym does not indicate generality (umum).
2
Apply to omission of commanded acts
If someone forgets to perform a commanded act (e.g., prayer, fasting, zakat), the worldly ruling is that it must be made up (qada). The reward is lost, but the obligation remains.
Examples in the passage: forgetting prayer, fasting, pilgrimage, zakat, expiation, or vow all require qada.
3
Apply to commission of prohibited acts
If someone forgetfully performs a prohibited act, the worldly ruling (like invalidity of worship, penalty) may or may not apply depending on whether there is a reminder (mudhakkir) and a compelling reason (dā'iyah). The passage gives a rule from al-Tahrir: if there is a reminder and no compelling reason, the worldly ruling remains (e.g., eating in prayer invalidates); if there is no reminder but a compelling reason, the worldly ruling drops (e.g., eating while fasting does not break fast).
The exceptions: forgetful eating while fasting, forgetful sexual intercourse while fasting are excused; forgetful eating in prayer is not excused.
4
Recognize the impact on penalties and liability
For forgetfulness in prohibited acts that entail a penalty (hadd, kaffarah), the forgetfulness creates a doubt (shubha) that may prevent the penalty. For financial liability, forgetfulness may excuse it only in certain cases like debt from sale/loan but not from usurpation.
Also: forgetfulness in divorce and manumission is treated like intentional; but in oaths, forgetfulness and intention are equal (so no excuse).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قال الأصوليون: إنه من باب ترك الحقيقة بدلالة محل الكلام لأن عين الخطأ وأخويه غير مرفوع، فالمراد حكمها وهو نوعان أُخروي وهو المأثم، ودنيوي وهو الفساد والحكمان مختلفان فصار الحكم بعد كونه مجازا مشتركا
فلا يعم .
لا
أما عندنا فلأن المشترك لا عموم له وأما عند الشافعي رحمه الله فلأن المجاز عموم له فإذا ثبت الأخروي إجماعا لم يثبت الآخر كذا في التنقيح، وتمامه في شرحنا على المنار .

(انتهى) .
ومن مسائل النسيان لو نسي المديون الدين حتى مات، فإن كان ثمن مبيع قرض لم يؤاخذ به، وإن كان غصبًا يؤاخذ به، كذا في الخانية.
أو منا لو علم الوصي بأن الموصي أوصى بوصايا لكنه نسي مقدارها وحكمه في وصايا خزانة المفتين.
(۱) رواه ابن ماجه في كتاب الطلاق باب ١٦.
For sin absolutely, due to the good hadith: 'Indeed Allah has put away from my Ummah error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced upon.' The usul scholars said: It is from the chapter of leaving the literal meaning due to the indication of the context, because the essence of error and its two siblings is not lifted; rather, what is intended is its ruling, which is of two types: otherworldly, which is sin, and worldly, which is invalidity. And the two rulings are different, so the ruling, after being a metaphor, becomes homonymous (mushtarak), thus it does not include both. No. As for us (Hanafis), because a homonym has no generality. As for al-Shafi'i, because a metaphor has generality. So when the otherworldly [ruling] is proven by consensus, the other is not established. Thus in al-Tanqih, and its completion in our commentary on al-Manar. As for the worldly ruling: if it occurs in the omission of a commanded act, it is not dropped but must be made up, and the reward for it is not obtained; or if it occurs in a prohibited act, if it necessitates a penalty, it is a doubt (shubha) in dropping it. So whoever forgets a prayer, fasting, pilgrimage, zakat, expiation, or a vow, must make it up without fear. Similarly, standing at other than Arafah by mistake requires making up unanimously. Among them: one who prays with a preventing impurity forgetfully, or forgets a pillar of the prayer, or is certain of error in ijtihad concerning water and garment at the time of prayer and fasting, or forgets the intention of fasting, or speaks in prayer forgetfully. And from what drops its ruling in forgetfulness: if one eats or drinks forgetfully while fasting, or has intercourse, it does not break [the fast]; but if one eats forgetfully in prayer, it invalidates. If one says the salam forgetfully in a four-rakat prayer after two rakats, the forgetful and intentional are the same in oaths. Similarly in divorce: if one says 'My wife is divorced' forgetfully, thinking he has no wife, similarly in manumission, and similarly in the prohibitions of ihram. And an origin for this was made in al-Tahrir: if there is a reminder (mudhakkir) and no compelling reason (da'iyah), like the eating of the praying person, it does not drop due to his negligence; unlike his salam while sitting. Or if there is no reminder with a compelling reason, like the fasting person's eating, it drops; or if neither, then even more so, like the butcher leaving the tasmiyah. (End). And from the issues of forgetfulness: if the debtor forgets the debt until he dies, then if it was the price of a sale or a loan, he is not taken to account; but if it was usurpation (ghasb), he is taken to account. Thus in al-Khaniyyah. Or among them: if the executor (wasi) knows that the testator gave bequests but he forgets their amount, the ruling is in Wasaaya Khizanat al-Muftin. (1) Narrated by Ibn Majah in the book of divorce, chapter 16.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
The passage begins by citing the well-known hadith that Allah has lifted sin from the community for error, forgetfulness, and coercion. The author then explains the usul scholars' interpretation: the literal meaning (the acts themselves) are not removed; rather, the legal rulings are removed. The rulings are of two types: otherworldly (sin/punishment in the afterlife) and worldly (invalidity or liability in this world). Since the word 'hukm' after being metaphorical becomes homonymous (mushtarak), it cannot include both meanings; the Hanafi position is that a homonym has no generality, so it does not cover both. The Shafi'i argument that a metaphor has generality is rejected; the other worldly ruling is removed by consensus, but the worldly ruling remains. The passage then details the worldly implications of forgetfulness. For commanded acts, forgetfulness does not remove the obligation to make up; the reward is lost but the duty remains. For prohibited acts, if a penalty (like hadd or kaffarah) applies, forgetfulness creates a doubt (shubha) that may prevent the penalty. Examples: forgetting to pray, fast, etc. require making up; forgetting a pillar of prayer or speaking in prayer invalidates it; but eating forgetfully in Ramadan does not break the fast. The author provides a key principle from al-Tahrir: if there is a reminder (a factor that should have reminded the person, like being in prayer) and no compelling external reason, forgetfulness does not excuse (e.g., eating in prayer). If there is no reminder but there is a compelling reason (e.g., hunger while fasting), it excuses. If there is no reminder and no compelling reason, it excuses even more (e.g., butcher forgetting tasmiyah). Additional issues: a debtor who forgets his debt is only liable if it was due to usurpation; an executor forgetting the amount of bequests has rulings detailed in other works. This passage establishes the nuanced Hanafi position that forgetfulness is an excuse only for sin, not for worldly obligations or invalidations, except in specific cases where there is genuine lack of awareness and no negligence.

Key Terms
النسيان (al-nisyān): Forgetfulness: an involuntary lapse of memory that is excused from sin but not necessarily from worldly legal consequences.
الحكم الدنيوي (al-hukm al-dunyawī): Worldly ruling: includes obligation of qada, invalidation of acts, liability for damages.
المشترك (al-mushtarak): Homonym: a word with multiple unrelated meanings; in usul, it has no general application to all meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a person who forgetfully eats while fasting in Ramadan?
A) The fast is broken and must be made up with kaffarah.
B) The fast is invalid and must be made up without kaffarah.
C) The fast remains valid and no make-up is required.
D) The fast remains valid, but it is recommended to make it up.

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness lifts sin but not worldly rulings; exceptions depend on the presence of a reminder and a compelling reason.
